	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Civil Rights Act of 1964</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><span aria-disabled='false' title='Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page.' class='flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget'></span><span id='mw-fr-revisiontoggle' aria-disabled='false' title='show/hide details' class='fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget'></span></div>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Green_v._Brennan" title="Green v. Brennan">Green v. Brennan</a></i> (2016)</li></ul>

<p>The <b>Civil Rights Act of 1964</b> (<a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-88-352">88–352</a>, 78&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-78-241">241</a>, enacted July&#160;2, 1964) is a landmark <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil rights">civil rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_labor_law" title="United States labor law">labor law</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> that outlaws discrimination based on race, color, religion, sex, or national origin.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It prohibits unequal application of voter registration requirements, and <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> in schools, employment, and <a href="/wiki/Public_accommodations" class="mw-redirect" title="Public accommodations">public accommodations</a>.
</p><p>Initially, powers given to enforce the act were weak, but these were supplemented during later years. Congress asserted its authority to legislate under several different parts of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, principally its power to regulate <a href="/wiki/Interstate_commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="Interstate commerce">interstate commerce</a> under <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">Article One</a> (section 8), its duty to guarantee all citizens <a href="/wiki/Equal_Protection_Clause" title="Equal Protection Clause">equal protection</a> of the laws under the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_U.S._Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a>, and its duty to protect voting rights under the <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifteenth Amendment</a>.
</p><p>The legislation had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> in June 1963, but opposed by filibuster in the Senate. After <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">Kennedy was assassinated</a> on November 22, 1963, President <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> pushed the bill forward, which in its final form was passed in the U.S. Congress by a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> vote of 73–27 and <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House</a> vote of 289–126. The Act was signed into law by President Johnson on July 2, 1964, at the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 1883 landmark <i><a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Cases" title="Civil Rights Cases">Civil Rights Cases</a></i> the United States Supreme Court had ruled that Congress did not have the power to prohibit discrimination in the private sector, thus stripping the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a> of much of its ability to protect civil rights.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 19th and early 20th century, the legal justification for voiding the Civil Rights Act of 1875 was part of a larger trend by members of the United States Supreme Court to invalidate most government regulations of the private sector, except when dealing with laws designed to protect traditional public morality.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights,_11_June_1963.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/170px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/255px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg/340px-President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights%2C_11_June_1963.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="963" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Kennedy_addresses_nation_on_Civil_Rights,_11_June_1963.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John F. Kennedy addresses the nation about civil rights on June 11, 1963</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1930s, during the New Deal, the majority of the Supreme Court justices gradually shifted their legal theory to allow for greater government regulation of the private sector under the commerce clause, thus paving the way for the Federal government to enact civil rights laws prohibiting both public and private sector discrimination on the basis of the commerce clause.
</p><p>The 1964 bill was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Report_to_the_American_People_on_Civil_Rights" title="Report to the American People on Civil Rights">Report to the American People on Civil Rights</a> on June 11, 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy sought legislation "giving all Americans the right to be served in facilities which are open to the public—hotels, restaurants, theaters, retail stores, and similar establishments"—as well as "greater protection for the right to vote". Kennedy delivered this speech in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_campaign" title="Birmingham campaign">Birmingham campaign</a> and the growing number of demonstrations and protests throughout the southern United States. Kennedy was moved to action following the elevated racial tensions and wave of black riots in the spring 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Emulating the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">Civil Rights Act of 1875</a>, Kennedy's civil rights bill included provisions to ban discrimination in public accommodations, and to enable the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">U.S. Attorney General</a> to join in lawsuits against state governments which operated segregated school systems, among other provisions. However, it did not include a number of provisions deemed essential by civil rights leaders, including protection against police brutality, ending discrimination in private employment, or granting the Justice Department power to initiate desegregation or job discrimination lawsuits.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Representatives">House of Representatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: House of Representatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Civilrightsact1964.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Civilrightsact1964.jpg/220px-Civilrightsact1964.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Civilrightsact1964.jpg/330px-Civilrightsact1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Civilrightsact1964.jpg/440px-Civilrightsact1964.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="4136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Civilrightsact1964.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the Civil Rights Act of 1964</div></div></div>
<p>On June 11, 1963, President Kennedy met with Republican leaders to discuss the legislation before his television address to the nation that evening. Two days later, <a href="/wiki/Senate_Minority_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Minority Leader">Senate Minority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Everett_Dirksen" title="Everett Dirksen">Everett Dirksen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Senate_Majority_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Majority Leader">Senate Majority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mansfield" title="Mike Mansfield">Mike Mansfield</a> both voiced support for the president's bill, except for provisions guaranteeing equal access to places of public accommodations. This led to several Republican Representatives drafting a compromise bill to be considered. On June 19, the president sent his bill to Congress as it was originally written, saying legislative action was "imperative".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The president's bill went first to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a>, where it was referred to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_the_Judiciary" title="United States House Committee on the Judiciary">Judiciary Committee</a>, chaired by <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Celler" title="Emanuel Celler">Emanuel Celler</a>, a Democrat from <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. After a series of hearings on the bill, Celler's committee strengthened the act, adding provisions to ban racial discrimination in employment, providing greater protection to black voters, eliminating segregation in all publicly owned facilities (not just schools), and strengthening the anti-segregation clauses regarding public facilities such as lunch counters. They also added authorization for the Attorney General to file lawsuits to protect individuals against the deprivation of any rights secured by the Constitution or U.S. law. In essence, this was the controversial "Title III" that had been removed from the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1957" title="Civil Rights Act of 1957">1957 Act</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1960" title="Civil Rights Act of 1960">1960 Act</a>. Civil rights organizations pressed hard for this provision because it could be used to protect peaceful protesters and black voters from police brutality and suppression of free speech rights.
</p><p>Kennedy called the congressional leaders to the White House in late October 1963 to line up the necessary votes in the House for passage.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The bill was reported out of the Judiciary Committee in November 1963 and referred to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Rules" title="United States House Committee on Rules">Rules Committee</a>, whose chairman, <a href="/wiki/Howard_W._Smith" title="Howard W. Smith">Howard W. Smith</a>, a Democrat and staunch segregationist from <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, indicated his intention to keep the bill bottled up indefinitely.
</p>
<h3><span id="Johnson.27s_appeal_to_Congress"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Johnson's_appeal_to_Congress">Johnson's appeal to Congress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Johnson&#039;s appeal to Congress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_John_F._Kennedy" title="Assassination of John F. Kennedy">assassination of John F. Kennedy</a> on November 22, 1963, changed the political situation. Kennedy's successor as president, <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a>, made use of his experience in legislative politics, along with the <a href="/wiki/Bully_pulpit" title="Bully pulpit">bully pulpit</a> he wielded as president, in support of the bill. In his first address to a <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">joint session of Congress</a> on November 27, 1963, Johnson told the legislators, "No memorial oration or eulogy could more eloquently honor President Kennedy's memory than the earliest possible passage of the civil rights bill for which he fought so long."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Judiciary Committee chairman Celler filed a <a href="/wiki/Discharge_petition" title="Discharge petition">petition to discharge</a> the bill from the Rules Committee; it required the support of a majority of House members to move the bill to the floor. Initially Celler had a difficult time acquiring the signatures necessary, with many Representatives who supported the civil rights bill itself remaining cautious about violating normal House procedure with the rare use of a discharge petition. By the time of the 1963 winter recess, 50 signatures were still needed.
</p><p>After the return of Congress from its winter recess, however, it was apparent that public opinion in the North favored the bill and that the petition would acquire the necessary signatures. To avert the humiliation of a successful discharge petition, Chairman Smith relented and allowed the bill to pass through the Rules Committee.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lobbying_efforts">Lobbying efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Lobbying efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lobbying support for the Civil Rights Act was coordinated by the <a href="/wiki/Leadership_Conference_on_Civil_and_Human_Rights" title="Leadership Conference on Civil and Human Rights">Leadership Conference on Civil Rights</a>, a coalition of 70 liberal and labor organizations. The principal lobbyists for the Leadership Conference were civil rights lawyer <a href="/wiki/Joseph_L._Rauh_Jr." title="Joseph L. Rauh Jr.">Joseph L. Rauh Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Mitchell_Jr." title="Clarence Mitchell Jr.">Clarence Mitchell Jr.</a> of the NAACP.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Passage_in_the_Senate">Passage in the Senate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Passage in the Senate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/200px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/300px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg/400px-MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2183" data-file-height="2392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MLK_and_Malcolm_X_USNWR_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_X" title="Malcolm X">Malcolm X</a> at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a> on March 26, 1964. Both had come to hear the Senate debate on the bill. This was the only time the two men ever met; their meeting lasted only one minute.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Johnson, who wanted the bill passed as soon as possible, ensured that the bill would be quickly considered by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a>. Normally, the bill would have been referred to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_the_Judiciary" title="United States Senate Committee on the Judiciary">Senate Judiciary Committee</a>, chaired by Senator <a href="/wiki/James_O._Eastland" class="mw-redirect" title="James O. Eastland">James O. Eastland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>. Given Eastland's firm opposition, it seemed impossible that the bill would reach the Senate floor. <a href="/wiki/Senate_Majority_Leader" class="mw-redirect" title="Senate Majority Leader">Senate Majority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mansfield" title="Mike Mansfield">Mike Mansfield</a> took a novel approach to prevent the bill from being relegated to Judiciary Committee limbo. Having initially waived a second reading of the bill, which would have led to it being immediately referred to the Judiciary, Mansfield gave the bill a second reading on February 26, 1964, and then proposed, in the absence of precedent for instances when a second reading did not immediately follow the first, that the bill bypass the Judiciary Committee and immediately be sent to the Senate floor for debate.
</p><p>When the bill came before the full Senate for debate on March 30, 1964, the "Southern Bloc" of 18 southern Democratic Senators and one Republican Senator led by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Russell,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Russell, Jr.">Richard Russell</a> (D-GA) launched a <a href="/wiki/Filibuster" title="Filibuster">filibuster</a> to prevent its passage.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Said Russell: "We will resist to the bitter end any measure or any movement which would have a tendency to bring about <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">social equality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">intermingling and amalgamation of the races</a> in our (Southern) states."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act,_July_2,_1964.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/220px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/330px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg/440px-Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act%2C_July_2%2C_1964.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7000" data-file-height="4687" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lyndon_Johnson_signing_Civil_Rights_Act,_July_2,_1964.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> signs the Civil Rights Act of 1964. Among the guests behind him is <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a></div></div></div>
<p>Strong opposition to the bill also came from Senator <a href="/wiki/Strom_Thurmond" title="Strom Thurmond">Strom Thurmond</a> (D-SC): "This so-called Civil Rights Proposals, which the President has sent to Capitol Hill for enactment into law, are unconstitutional, unnecessary, unwise and extend beyond the realm of reason. This is the worst civil-rights package ever presented to the Congress and is reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a> proposals and actions of the <a href="/wiki/Radical_Republicans" title="Radical Republicans">radical Republican</a> Congress."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>

<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_Civil_Rights_signing_1964_edited.ogg" title="File:LBJ Civil Rights signing 1964 edited.ogg">"Remarks upon Signing the Civil Rights Act of 1964"</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="621.6025170068" data-startoffset="2.7472108843537" data-mwtitle="LBJ_Civil_Rights_signing_1964_edited.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b2/LBJ_Civil_Rights_signing_1964_edited.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (94 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="94154" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b2/LBJ_Civil_Rights_signing_1964_edited.ogg/LBJ_Civil_Rights_signing_1964_edited.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="134688" /></audio></div></div>
