<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>After 54 days of filibuster, Senators <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> (D-MN), <a href="/wiki/Mike_Mansfield" title="Mike Mansfield">Mike Mansfield</a> (D-MT), <a href="/wiki/Everett_Dirksen" title="Everett Dirksen">Everett Dirksen</a> (R-IL), and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuchel" title="Thomas Kuchel">Thomas Kuchel</a> (R-CA),  introduced a substitute bill that they hoped would attract enough Republican swing votes in addition to the core liberal Democrats behind the legislation to end the filibuster. The compromise bill was weaker than the House version in regard to government power to regulate the conduct of private business, but it was not so weak as to cause the House to reconsider the legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the morning of June 10, 1964, Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_Byrd" title="Robert Byrd">Robert Byrd</a> (D-W.Va.) completed a filibustering address that he had begun 14 hours and 13 minutes earlier opposing the legislation. Until then, the measure had occupied the Senate for 60 working days, including six Saturdays. A day earlier, Democratic Whip <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> of Minnesota, the bill's manager, concluded he had the 67 votes required at that time to end the debate and end the filibuster. With six wavering senators providing a four-vote victory margin, the final tally stood at 71 to 29. Never in history had the Senate been able to muster enough votes to cut off a filibuster on a civil rights bill. And only once in the 37 years since 1927 had it agreed to <a href="/wiki/Cloture" title="Cloture">cloture</a> for any measure.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 19, the substitute (compromise) bill passed the Senate by a vote of 73–27, and quickly passed through the House–Senate <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress_Conference_committee" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Congress Conference committee">conference committee</a>, which adopted the Senate version of the bill. The conference bill was passed by both houses of Congress, and was signed into law by President Johnson on July 2, 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vote_totals">Vote totals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vote totals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Totals are in "<i>Yea</i>–<i>Nay</i>" format:
</p>
<ul><li>The original House version: 290–130 &#160; (69–31%)</li>
<li>Cloture in the Senate: 71–29 &#160; (71–29%)</li>
<li>The Senate version: 73–27 &#160; (73–27%)</li>
<li>The Senate version, as voted on by the House: 289–126 &#160; (70–30%)</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_party">By party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: By party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg/200px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg/300px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg/400px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg 2x" data-file-width="466" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-HR7152.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The record of the <a href="/wiki/Roll_call_vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Roll call vote">roll call vote</a> kept by the <a href="/wiki/House_Clerk" class="mw-redirect" title="House Clerk">House Clerk</a> on final passage of the bill</div></div></div>
<p>The original House version:<sup id="cite_ref-King_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Democratic Party: 152–96 &#160; (61–39%)</li>
<li>Republican Party: 138–34 &#160; (80–20%)</li></ul>
<p>Cloture in the Senate:<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Democratic Party: 44–23 &#160; (66–34%)</li>
<li>Republican Party: 27–6 &#160; (82–18%)</li></ul>
<p>The Senate version:<sup id="cite_ref-King_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Democratic Party: 46–21 &#160; (69–31%)</li>
<li>Republican Party: 27–6 &#160; (82–18%)</li></ul>
<p>The Senate version, voted on by the House:<sup id="cite_ref-King_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-King-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Democratic Party: 153–91 &#160; (63–37%)</li>
<li>Republican Party: 136–35 &#160; (80–20%)</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="By_party_and_region">By party and region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: By party and region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><b>Note:</b> "Southern", as used in this section, refers to members of Congress from the eleven states that had made up the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. "Northern" refers to members from the other 39 states, regardless of the geographic location of those states.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The original House version:
</p>
<ul><li>Southern Democrats: 7–87 &#160; (7–93%)</li>
<li>Southern Republicans: 0–10 &#160; (0–100%)</li>
<li>Northern Democrats: 145–9 &#160; (94–6%)</li>
<li>Northern Republicans: 138–24 &#160; (85–15%)</li></ul>
<p>The Senate version:
</p>
<ul><li>Southern Democrats: 1–20 &#160; (5–95%) (only <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Yarborough" title="Ralph Yarborough">Ralph Yarborough</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> voted in favor)</li>
<li>Southern Republicans: 0–1 &#160; (0–100%) (<a href="/wiki/John_Tower" title="John Tower">John Tower</a> of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>)</li>
<li>Northern Democrats: 45–1 &#160; (98–2%) (only <a href="/wiki/Robert_Byrd" title="Robert Byrd">Robert Byrd</a> of <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a> voted against)</li>
<li>Northern Republicans: 27–5 &#160; (84–16%)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aspects">Aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Women.27s_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_rights">Women's rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Women&#039;s rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg/220px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg/330px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg/440px-CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="342" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CivilRightsAct1964-HouseRollCall-Sex-Amendment.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engrossing copy of H.R. 7152, which added sex to the categories of persons against whom the bill prohibited discrimination, as passed by the House of Representatives<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Just one year earlier, the same Congress had passed the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_of_1963" title="Equal Pay Act of 1963">Equal Pay Act of 1963</a>, which prohibited wage differentials based on sex. The prohibition on sex discrimination was added to the Civil Rights Act by <a href="/wiki/Howard_W._Smith" title="Howard W. Smith">Howard W. Smith</a>, a powerful Virginia Democrat who chaired the House Rules Committee and who strongly opposed the legislation. Smith's amendment was passed by a teller vote of 168 to 133. Historians debate Smith's motivation, whether it was a <a href="/wiki/Wrecking_amendment" title="Wrecking amendment">cynical attempt to defeat the bill</a> by someone opposed to civil rights both for blacks and women, or an attempt to support their rights by broadening the bill to include women.<sup id="cite_ref-Freeman_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Smith expected that Republicans, who had included <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women&#39;s rights">equal rights for women</a> in their party's platform since 1944,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> would probably vote for the amendment. Historians speculate that Smith was trying to embarrass northern Democrats who opposed civil rights for women because the clause was opposed by labor unions. Representative <a href="/wiki/Carl_Elliott" title="Carl Elliott">Carl Elliott</a> of Alabama later claimed, "Smith didn't give a damn about women's rights...he was trying to knock off votes either then or down the line because there was always a hard core of men who didn't favor women's rights,"<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Congressional_Record" title="Congressional Record">Congressional Record</a></i> records that Smith was greeted by laughter when he introduced the amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-Gold_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Smith asserted that he was not joking; he sincerely supported the amendment and, indeed, along with Rep. <a href="/wiki/Martha_Griffiths" title="Martha Griffiths">Martha Griffiths</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> he was the chief spokesperson for the amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-Gold_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gold-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> For twenty years Smith had sponsored the Equal Rights Amendment (with no linkage to racial issues) in the House because he believed in it. He for decades had been close to the <a href="/wiki/National_Woman%27s_Party" title="National Woman&#39;s Party">National Woman's Party</a> and its leader <a href="/wiki/Alice_Paul" title="Alice Paul">Alice Paul</a>, who was also the leader in winning the right to vote for women in 1920, the author of the first Equal Rights Amendment, and a chief supporter of equal rights proposals since then. She and other feminists had worked with Smith since 1945 trying to find a way to include sex as a protected civil rights category. Now was the moment.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Griffiths argued that the new law would protect black women but not white women, and that was unfair to white women. Furthermore, she argued that the laws "protecting" women from unpleasant jobs were actually designed to enable men to monopolize those jobs, and that was unfair to women who were not allowed to try out for those jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The amendment passed with the votes of Republicans and Southern Democrats. The final law passed with the votes of Republicans and Northern Democrats. Thus, as Justice <a href="/wiki/William_Rehnquist" title="William Rehnquist">William Rehnquist</a> explained in <i><a href="/wiki/Meritor_Savings_Bank_v._Vinson" title="Meritor Savings Bank v. Vinson">Meritor Savings Bank v. Vinson</a></i>, "The prohibition against discrimination based on sex was added to Title VII at the last minute on the floor of the House of Representatives... the bill quickly passed as amended, and we are left with little legislative history to guide us in interpreting the Act's prohibition against discrimination based on 'sex.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Desegregation">Desegregation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Desegregation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>One of the most damaging arguments by the bill's opponents was that once passed, the bill would require <a href="/wiki/Desegregation_busing_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Desegregation busing in the United States">forced busing</a> to achieve certain <a href="/wiki/Racial_quotas" class="mw-redirect" title="Racial quotas">racial quotas</a> in schools.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_251_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_251-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Proponents of the bill, such as <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Celler" title="Emanuel Celler">Emanuel Celler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Javits" title="Jacob Javits">Jacob Javits</a>, said that the bill would not authorize such measures. Leading sponsor Senator <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a> (D-MN) wrote two amendments specifically designed to outlaw busing.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_251_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_251-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Humphrey said "if the bill were to compel it, it would be a violation [of the Constitution], because it would be handling the matter on the basis of race and we would be transporting children because of race."<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_251_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_251-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> While Javits said any government official who sought to use the bill for busing purposes "would be making a fool of himself," two years later the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health,_Education_and_Welfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Health, Education and Welfare">Department of Health, Education and Welfare</a> said that Southern school districts would be required to meet mathematical ratios of students by busing.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_251_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_251-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction">Reaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_repercussions">Political repercussions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Political repercussions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Realigning_election" title="Realigning election">Realigning election</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg/200px-LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg/300px-LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg/400px-LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8578" data-file-height="8380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_Civil_Rights_Act_crowd.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Johnson speaks to a television camera at the signing of the Civil Rights Act</div></div></div>
<p>The bill divided and engendered a long-term change in the demographic support of both parties. President Johnson realized that supporting this bill would risk losing the South's overwhelming support of the Democratic Party. Both Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Kennedy">Robert Kennedy</a> and Vice President Johnson had pushed for the introduction of the civil rights legislation. Johnson told Kennedy aide <a href="/wiki/Ted_Sorensen" title="Ted Sorensen">Ted Sorensen</a> that "I know the risks are great and we might lose the South, but those sorts of states may be lost anyway."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/Richard_Russell,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Russell, Jr.">Richard Russell, Jr.</a> later warned President Johnson that his strong support for the civil rights bill "will not only cost you the South, it will cost you the election".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Johnson, however, went on to win the <a href="/wiki/1964_United_States_presidential_election" title="1964 United States presidential election">1964 election</a> by one of the biggest landslides in American history. The South, which had five states swing Republican in 1964, became a stronghold of the Republican Party by the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although majorities in both parties voted for the bill, there were notable exceptions. Though he opposed forced segregation,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> Senator <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> of Arizona voted against the bill, remarking, "You can't legislate morality." Goldwater had supported previous attempts to pass civil rights legislation in 1957 and 1960 as well as the <a href="/wiki/24th_Amendment" class="mw-redirect" title="24th Amendment">24th Amendment</a> outlawing the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Poll tax (United States)">poll tax</a>. He stated that the reason for his opposition to the 1964 bill was Title II, which in his opinion violated individual liberty and <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States&#39; rights">states' rights</a>. Democrats and Republicans from the Southern states opposed the bill and led an unsuccessful 83-day filibuster, including Senators <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gore,_Sr." class="mw-redirect" title="Albert Gore, Sr.">Albert Gore, Sr.</a> (D-TN) and <a href="/wiki/J._William_Fulbright" title="J. William Fulbright">J. William Fulbright</a> (D-AR), as well as Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_Byrd" title="Robert Byrd">Robert Byrd</a> (D-WV), who personally filibustered for 14 hours straight.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Continued_resistance">Continued resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Continued resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There were white business owners who claimed that Congress did not have the constitutional authority to ban segregation in public accommodations. For example, Moreton Rolleston, the owner of a motel in Atlanta, Georgia, said he should not be forced to serve black travelers, saying, "the fundamental question [...] is whether or not Congress has the power to take away the liberty of an individual to run his business as he sees fit in the selection and choice of his customers".<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Rolleston claimed that the Civil Rights Act of 1964 was a breach of the <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a> and also violated the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fifth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth</a> Amendments by depriving him of "liberty and property without due process".<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Atlanta_Motel_v._United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart of Atlanta Motel v. United States">Heart of Atlanta Motel v. United States</a></i> (1964), the Supreme Court held that Congress drew its authority from the Constitution's Commerce Clause, rejecting Rolleston's claims.
</p><p>Resistance to the public accommodation clause continued for years on the ground, especially in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> When local college students in Orangeburg, South Carolina, attempted to desegregate a bowling alley in 1968, they were violently attacked, leading to rioting and what became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Orangeburg_massacre" title="Orangeburg massacre">Orangeburg massacre</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Resistance by school boards continued into the next decade, with the most significant declines in black-white school segregation only occurring at the end of the 1960s and the start of the 1970s in the aftermath of the <i><a href="/wiki/Green_v._County_School_Board_of_New_Kent_County" title="Green v. County School Board of New Kent County">Green v. County School Board of New Kent County</a></i> (1968) court decision.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Titles">Titles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Titles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>(The full text of the Act is available <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.ourdocuments.gov/doc.php?flash=true&amp;doc=97&amp;page=transcript">online</a>.)
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_I.E2.80.94voting_rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_I—voting_rights">Title I—voting rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Title I—voting rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>This title barred unequal application of voter registration requirements. Title I did not eliminate <a href="/wiki/Literacy_test" title="Literacy test">literacy tests</a>, which acted as one barrier for black voters, other racial minorities, and poor whites in the South or address economic retaliation, police repression, or physical violence against nonwhite voters. While the Act did require that voting rules and procedures be applied equally to all races, it did not abolish the concept of voter "qualification". It accepted the idea that citizens do not have an automatic right to vote but would have to meet standards beyond citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Voting Rights Act">Voting Rights Act</a> of 1965 directly addressed and eliminated most voting qualifications beyond citizenship.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_II.E2.80.94public_accommodations"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_II—public_accommodations">Title II—public accommodations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Title II—public accommodations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Outlawed discrimination based on race, color, religion, or national origin in hotels, motels, restaurants, theaters, and all other public accommodations engaged in interstate commerce; exempted private clubs without defining the term "private".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_III.E2.80.94desegregation_of_public_facilities"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_III—desegregation_of_public_facilities">Title III—desegregation of public facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Title III—desegregation of public facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prohibited state and municipal governments from denying access to public facilities on grounds of race, color, religion, or national origin.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_IV.E2.80.94desegregation_of_public_education"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_IV—desegregation_of_public_education">Title IV—desegregation of public education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Title IV—desegregation of public education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Enforced the desegregation of public schools and authorized the U.S. Attorney General to file suits to enforce said act.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_V.E2.80.94Commission_on_Civil_Rights"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_V—Commission_on_Civil_Rights">Title V—Commission on Civil Rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Title V—Commission on Civil Rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Expanded the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Commission_on_Civil_Rights" title="United States Commission on Civil Rights">Civil Rights Commission</a> established by the earlier <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1957" title="Civil Rights Act of 1957">Civil Rights Act of 1957</a> with additional powers, rules and procedures.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_VI.E2.80.94nondiscrimination_in_federally_assisted_programs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_VI—nondiscrimination_in_federally_assisted_programs">Title VI—nondiscrimination in federally assisted programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Title VI—nondiscrimination in federally assisted programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prevents discrimination by programs and activities that receive federal funds. If a recipient of federal funds is found in violation of Title VI, that recipient may lose its federal funding.
</p><p><b>General</b>
</p><p>This title declares it to be the policy of the United States that discrimination on the ground of race, color, or national origin shall not occur in connection with programs and activities receiving Federal financial assistance and authorizes and directs the appropriate Federal departments and agencies to take action to carry out this policy. This title is not intended to apply to foreign assistance programs.
Section 601&#160;&#8211;&#32;This section states the general principle that no person in the United States shall be excluded from participation in or otherwise discriminated against on the ground of race, color, or national origin under any program or activity receiving Federal financial assistance.
</p><p>Section 602 directs each Federal agency administering a program of Federal financial assistance by way of grant, contract, or loan to take action pursuant to rule, regulation, or order of general applicability to effectuate the principle of section 601 in a manner consistent with the achievement of the objectives of the statute authorizing the assistance. In seeking the effect compliance with its requirements imposed under this section, an agency is authorized to terminate or to refuse to grant or to continue assistance under a program to any recipient as to whom there has been an express finding pursuant to a hearing of a failure to comply with the requirements under that program, and it may also employ any other means authorized by law. However, each agency is directed first to seek compliance with its requirements by voluntary means.
</p><p>Section 603 provides that any agency action taken pursuant to section 602 shall be subject to such judicial review as would be available for similar actions by that agency on other grounds. Where the agency action consists of terminating or refusing to grant or to continue financial assistance because of a finding of a failure of the recipient to comply with the agency's requirements imposed under section 602, and the agency action would not otherwise be subject to judicial review under existing law, judicial review shall nevertheless be available to any person aggrieved as provided in section 10 of the Administrative Procedure Act (<a href="/wiki/Title_5_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 5 of the United States Code">5 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/5/1009">§&#160;1009</a>). The section also states explicitly that in the latter situation such agency action shall not be deemed committed to unreviewable agency discretion within the meaning of section 10. The purpose of this provision is to obviate the possible argument that although section 603 provides for review in accordance with section 10, section 10 itself has an exception for action "committed to agency discretion," which might otherwise be carried over into section 603. It is not the purpose of this provision of section 603, however, otherwise to alter the scope of judicial review as presently provided in section 10(e) of the Administrative Procedure Act.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_VII.E2.80.94equal_employment_opportunity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_VII—equal_employment_opportunity">Title VII—equal employment opportunity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Title VII—equal employment opportunity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_labor_law" title="United States labor law">United States labor law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Employment_discrimination_law_in_the_United_States" title="Employment discrimination law in the United States">Employment discrimination law in the United States</a></div>
<p>Title VII of the Act, codified as Subchapter VI of Chapter 21 of <a href="/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 42 of the United States Code">title 42 of the United States Code</a>, prohibits discrimination by covered employers on the basis of race, color, religion, sex or national origin (see <a href="/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 42 of the United States Code">42 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/2000e-2">§&#160;2000e-2</a><sup id="cite_ref-Finduslaw_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Finduslaw-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>). Title VII applies to and covers an employer "who has fifteen (15) or more employees for each working day in each of twenty or more calendar weeks in the current or preceding calendar year" as written in the Definitions section under <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/2000e">42 U.S.C. §2000e(b)</a>. Title VII also prohibits discrimination against an individual because of his or her association with another individual of a particular race, color, religion, sex, or national origin, such as by an interracial marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The EEO Title VII has also been supplemented with legislation prohibiting pregnancy, age, and disability discrimination (<i>See</i> <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_Discrimination#Pregnancy_Discrimination_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Pregnancy Discrimination">Pregnancy Discrimination Act of 1978</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_Discrimination_in_Employment_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Age Discrimination in Employment Act">Age Discrimination in Employment Act</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Americans_with_Disabilities_Act_of_1990" title="Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990">Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990</a>).
</p><p>In very narrowly defined situations, an employer is permitted to discriminate on the basis of a protected trait where the trait is a bona fide occupational qualification (BFOQ) reasonably necessary to the normal operation of that particular business or enterprise. To prove the <a href="/wiki/Bona_fide_occupational_qualifications" class="mw-redirect" title="Bona fide occupational qualifications">bona fide occupational qualifications</a> defense, an employer must prove three elements: a direct relationship between the protected trait and the ability to perform the duties of the job, the BFOQ relates to the "essence" or "central mission of the employer's business", and there is no less-restrictive or reasonable alternative (<i><a href="/wiki/United_Automobile_Workers_v._Johnson_Controls,_Inc." title="United Automobile Workers v. Johnson Controls, Inc.">United Automobile Workers v. Johnson Controls, Inc.</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_499" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 499">499</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/499/187/">187</a>&#32;(1991) 111 S.Ct. 1196). The Bona Fide Occupational Qualification exception is an extremely narrow exception to the general prohibition of discrimination based on protected traits (<i><a href="/wiki/Dothard_v._Rawlinson" title="Dothard v. Rawlinson">Dothard v. Rawlinson</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_433" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 433">433</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/433/321/">321</a>&#32;(1977) 97 S.Ct. 2720). An employer or customer's preference for an individual of a particular religion is not sufficient to establish a Bona Fide Occupational Qualification (<i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission_v._Kamehameha_School_%E2%80%94_Bishop_Estate&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission v. Kamehameha School — Bishop Estate (page does not exist)">Equal Employment Opportunity Commission v. Kamehameha School — Bishop Estate</a></i>, 990 F.2d 458 (9th Cir. 1993)).
</p><p>Title VII allows for any employer, labor organization, joint labor-management committee, or employment agency to bypass the "unlawful employment practice" for any person involved with the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist Party of the United States">Communist Party of the United States</a> or of any other organization required to register as a Communist-action or Communist-front organization by final order of the Subversive Activities Control Board pursuant to the <a href="/wiki/Subversive_Activities_Control_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Subversive Activities Control Act">Subversive Activities Control Act</a> of 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are partial and whole exceptions to Title VII for four types of employers:
</p>
<ul><li>Federal government; (Comment: The proscriptions against employment discrimination under Title VII are now applicable to certain federal government offices under <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://finduslaw.com/civil_rights_act_of_1964_cra_title_vii_equal_employment_opportunities_42_us_code_chapter_21#17">42 U.S.C. Section 2000e-16</a>)</li>
<li>Federally recognized Native American tribes<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Religious groups performing work connected to the group's activities, including associated education institutions;</li>
<li>Bona fide nonprofit private membership organizations</li></ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">Equal Employment Opportunity Commission</a> (EEOC) as well as certain <a href="/w/index.php?title=State_fair_employment_practices_agencies&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="State fair employment practices agencies (page does not exist)">state fair employment practices agencies</a> (FEPAs) enforce Title VII (see <a href="/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 42 of the United States Code">42 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/2000e-4">§&#160;2000e-4</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Finduslaw_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Finduslaw-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The EEOC and state FEPAs investigate, mediate, and may file lawsuits on behalf of employees. Where a state law is contradicted by a federal law, it is overridden.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Every state, except Arkansas and Mississippi, maintains a state FEPA (see EEOC and state FEPA <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.eeocoffice.com">directory </a>). Title VII also provides that an individual can bring a private lawsuit. An individual must file a complaint of discrimination with the EEOC within 180 days of learning of the discrimination or the individual may lose the right to file a lawsuit. Title VII only applies to employers who employ 15 or more employees for 20 or more weeks in the current or preceding calendar year (<a href="/wiki/Title_42_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 42 of the United States Code">42 U.S.C.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/42/2000e(b)">§&#160;2000e(b)</a>).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Precedents_and_history">Precedents and history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Precedents and history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the early 1980s, the EEOC and some federal courts began holding that <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> is also prohibited under the Act. In 1986, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> held in <i><a href="/wiki/Meritor_Savings_Bank_v._Vinson" title="Meritor Savings Bank v. Vinson">Meritor Savings Bank v. Vinson</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_477" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 477">477</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/477/57/">57</a>&#32;(1986), that sexual harassment is sex discrimination and is prohibited by Title VII. This case filed by plaintiff Mechelle Vinson was the first in the history of the court to recognize sexual harassment as actionable.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Following 1986, court cases in which the plaintiff suffers no economic loss can potentially argue for a violation of Title VII if the discrimination resulted in a hostile work environment.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Same-sex <a href="/wiki/Sexual_harassment" title="Sexual harassment">sexual harassment</a> has also been held in a unanimous decision written by <a href="/wiki/Justice_Scalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Justice Scalia">Justice Scalia</a> to be prohibited by Title VII (<i><a href="/wiki/Oncale_v._Sundowner_Offshore_Services,_Inc." title="Oncale v. Sundowner Offshore Services, Inc.">Oncale v. Sundowner Offshore Services, Inc.</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_523" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 523">523</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://supreme.justia.com/cases/federal/us/523/75/">75</a>&#32;(1998), 118 S.Ct. 998).
</p><p>In 2012, the EEOC ruled that employment discrimination on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a> status is prohibited under Title VII. The decision held that discrimination on the basis of gender identity qualified as discrimination on the basis of sex whether the discrimination was due to sex stereotyping, discomfort with the fact of an individual's transition, or discrimination due to a perceived change in the individual's sex.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the EEOC initiated two lawsuits against private companies for discrimination on the basis of gender identity, with additional litigation under consideration.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> As of November&#160;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Commissioner <a href="/wiki/Chai_Feldblum" title="Chai Feldblum">Chai Feldblum</a> is making an active effort to increase awareness of Title VII remedies for individuals discriminated on the basis of sexual orientation or gender identity.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 15, 2014, under a memorandum issued by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Holder" title="Eric Holder">Eric Holder</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> (DoJ) took a position that aligned with the EEOC, namely the prohibition of sex discrimination under Title VII encompassed the prohibition of discrimination based on gender identity or transgender status. DoJ had already stopped opposing claims of discrimination brought by federal transgender employees.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2017, Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Sessions" title="Jeff Sessions">Jeff Sessions</a> issued a directive that withdrew the Holder memorandum.<sup id="cite_ref-BF-171005_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-171005-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> According to a copy of the directive reviewed by <i>BuzzFeed News</i>, Sessions stated that Title VII should be narrowly interpreted to cover discrimination between "men and women".  Attorney General Session stated as a matter of law, "Title VII does not prohibit discrimination based on gender identity per se."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Devin O'Malley, speaking on behalf of the DoJ, stated "the last administration abandoned that fundamental principle [that the Department of Justice cannot expand the law beyond what Congress has provided], which necessitated today's action."  Sharon McGowan, a lawyer with Lambda Legal who previously served in the Civil Rights division of DoJ, rejected that argument, saying "[T]his memo is not actually a reflection of the law as it is — it's a reflection of what the DOJ wishes the law were" and "The Justice Department is actually getting back in the business of making anti-transgender law in court."<sup id="cite_ref-BF-171005_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BF-171005-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 11, 2017, the United States Supreme Court refused to hear an appeal in <i>Evans v. Georgia Regional Hospital</i>, in which a lower court ruled against the plaintiff, who had argued Title VII protections applied to sexual orientation. The 11th U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals stated in its earlier ruling that only the Supreme Court could determine if Title VII applied.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2019, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a> said they will hear three cases related to Title VII as it relates to the <a href="/wiki/LGBTQ_community" class="mw-redirect" title="LGBTQ community">LGBTQ community</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-title7lgbtq_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-title7lgbtq-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Two of the cases — <i>Bostock v. Clayton County, Georgia</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Zarda_v._Altitude_Express,_Inc." title="Zarda v. Altitude Express, Inc.">Altitude Express, Inc. v. Zarda</a></i> — were consolidated because both include claims that employers discriminated on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a>. The third — <i><a href="/wiki/R.G._%26_G.R._Harris_Funeral_Homes_Inc._v._Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="R.G. &amp; G.R. Harris Funeral Homes Inc. v. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">R.G. &amp; G.R. Harris Funeral Homes Inc. v. Equal Employment Opportunity Commission</a></i> — involves <a href="/wiki/Transgender" title="Transgender">transgender</a> employees.<sup id="cite_ref-title7lgbtq_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-title7lgbtq-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amicus_brief" class="mw-redirect" title="Amicus brief">Amicus briefs</a> submitted to the Supreme Court by the Trump administration argued courts nationwide should stop reading Title VII to protect the LGBTQ community as the intention of the act was only to prohibit unequal treatment between "biological sexes".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_VIII.E2.80.94registration_and_voting_statistics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_VIII—registration_and_voting_statistics">Title VIII—registration and voting statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Title VIII—registration and voting statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Required compilation of voter-registration and voting data in geographic areas specified by the Commission on Civil Rights.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_IX.E2.80.94intervention_and_removal_of_cases"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_IX—intervention_and_removal_of_cases">Title IX—intervention and removal of cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Title IX—intervention and removal of cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the prohibition of sex discrimination in federally funded education programs and activities, see <a href="/wiki/Title_IX" title="Title IX">Title IX of the Education Amendments Act of 1972</a>.</div>
<p>Title IX made it easier to move civil rights cases from state courts to federal court. This was of crucial importance to civil rights activists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2015)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> who contended that they could not get fair trials in state courts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_X.E2.80.94Community_Relations_Service"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_X—Community_Relations_Service">Title X—Community Relations Service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Title X—Community Relations Service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Established the <a href="/wiki/Community_Relations_Service" title="Community Relations Service">Community Relations Service</a>, tasked with assisting in community disputes involving claims of discrimination.
</p>
<h3><span id="Title_XI.E2.80.94miscellaneous"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_XI—miscellaneous">Title XI—miscellaneous</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Title XI—miscellaneous">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Title XI gives a defendant accused of certain categories of criminal contempt in a matter arising under title II, III, IV, V, VI, or VII of the Act the right to a jury trial. If convicted, the defendant can be fined an amount not to exceed $1,000 or imprisoned for not more than six months.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Amendments">Amendments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Amendments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Act_of_1972">Equal Employment Opportunity Act of 1972</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Equal Employment Opportunity Act of 1972">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Between 1965 and 1972, Title VII lacked any strong enforcement provisions. Instead, the Equal Employment Opportunity Commission was authorized only to investigate external claims of discrimination. The EEOC could then refer cases to the Justice Department for litigation if reasonable cause was found. The EEOC documented the nature and magnitude of discriminatory employment practices, the first study of this kind done.
</p><p>In 1972, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Act">Equal Employment Opportunity Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Act amended Title VII and gave EEOC authority to initiate its own enforcement litigation. The EEOC now played a major role in guiding judicial interpretations of civil rights legislation. The commission was also permitted for the first time to define "discrimination," a term excluded from the 1964 Act.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Case_law">Case law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Case law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Heart_of_Atlanta_Motel.2C_Inc._v._United_States"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_of_Atlanta_Motel,_Inc._v._United_States"><i>Heart of Atlanta Motel, Inc. v. United States</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Heart of Atlanta Motel, Inc. v. United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the Civil Rights Act of 1964 was passed, the Supreme Court upheld the law's application to the private sector, on the grounds that Congress has the power to regulate commerce between the States. The landmark case <i><a href="/wiki/Heart_of_Atlanta_Motel_v._United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart of Atlanta Motel v. United States">Heart of Atlanta Motel v. United States</a></i> established the constitutionality of the law, but it did not settle all of the legal questions surrounding the law.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phillips_v._Martin_Marietta_Corp."><i>Phillips v. Martin Marietta Corp.</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Phillips v. Martin Marietta Corp.">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Phillips_v._Martin_Marietta_Corp." title="Phillips v. Martin Marietta Corp.">Phillips v. Martin Marietta Corp.</a></i>, a 1971 Supreme Court case regarding the gender provisions of the Act, the Court ruled that a company could not discriminate against a potential female employee because she had a preschool-age child unless they did the same with potential male employees.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A federal court overruled an <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> state law that barred women from obtaining jobs which required the ability to lift 25 pounds and required women to take lunch breaks when men were not required to.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh_Press_Co._v._Pittsburgh_Commission_on_Human_Relations" title="Pittsburgh Press Co. v. Pittsburgh Commission on Human Relations">Pittsburgh Press Co. v. Pittsburgh Commission on Human Relations</a></i>, the United States Supreme Court decided that printing separate job listings for men and women was illegal, which ended that practice among the country's newspapers. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Service_Commission" title="United States Civil Service Commission">United States Civil Service Commission</a> ended the practice among federal jobs which designated them "women only" or "men only."<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lau_v._Nichols"><i>Lau v. Nichols</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Lau v. Nichols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1974 in the case <i><a href="/wiki/Lau_v._Nichols" title="Lau v. Nichols">Lau v. Nichols</a></i>, the Supreme Court ruled that the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> school district was violating non-English speaking students' rights under the 1964 act by placing them in regular classes rather than providing some sort of accommodation for them.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_270_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_270-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blake_v._City_of_Los_Angeles"><i>Blake v. City of Los Angeles</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Blake v. City of Los Angeles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1977, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Central_District_of_California" title="United States District Court for the Central District of California">U.S. District Court for the Central District of California</a> struck down state minimum height requirements for police officers as violating the Act; women usually could not meet these requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hively_v._Ivy_Tech_Community_College"><i>Hively v. Ivy Tech Community College</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Hively v. Ivy Tech Community College">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On April 4, 2017, in the case <i><a href="/wiki/Hively_v._Ivy_Tech_Community_College" title="Hively v. Ivy Tech Community College">Hively v. Ivy Tech Community College</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Seventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit</a> in Chicago, sitting <i><a href="/wiki/En_banc" title="En banc">en banc</a></i>, ruled that Title VII of the Act forbids discrimination on the basis of <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a> by a vote of 8–3.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>  Over the prior month, panels of both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Eleventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit</a> in Atlanta and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Second_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit</a> in New York City had reached the <a href="/wiki/Circuit_split" title="Circuit split">opposite conclusion</a>, finding that Title VII sex discrimination does not include claims based on sexual orientation.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_cases">Other cases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Other cases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1975, a federal civil rights agency warned a <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a> school that its end-of-year father-son and mother-daughter baseball games were illegal according to the 1964 Civil Rights Act.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> intervened, and the games were allowed to continue.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_245_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_245-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americans_with_Disabilities_Act_of_1990">Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Americans_with_Disabilities_Act_of_1990" title="Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990">Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990</a>—which has been called "the most important piece of federal legislation since the Civil Rights Act of 1964"—was influenced both by the structure and substance of the previous Civil Rights Act of 1964. The act was arguably of equal importance, and "draws substantially from the structure of that landmark legislation [Civil Rights Act of 1964]". The Americans with Disabilities Act paralleled its landmark predecessor structurally, drawing upon many of the same titles and statutes. For example, "Title I of the ADA, which bans employment discrimination by private employers on the basis of disability, parallels Title VII of the Act". Similarly, Title III of the Americans with Disabilities Act, "which proscribes discrimination on the basis of disability in public accommodations, tracks Title II of the 1964 Act while expanding upon the list of public accommodations covered." The Americans with Disabilities Act extended "the principle of nondiscrimination to people with disabilities",<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> an idea unsought in the United States before the passage of the Civil Rights Act of 1964. The Act also influenced later civil rights legislation, such as the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1968" title="Civil Rights Act of 1968">Civil Rights Act of 1968</a>, aiding not only African Americans, but also women.
</p>

<ul><li>Branch, Taylor (1998), <i>Pillar of Fire: America in the King Years 1963–65</i>, New York: Simon &amp; Schuster.</li>
